  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_1efcf70f-57d7-4d28-98cf-baabbcfd4d1e'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

  <span id="freeText13859466594006820484" style="display:none">If you're into stuff like this, <a href="https://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/1981/04/phallocracy-juliette-by-by-marquis-de.html" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read the full review.</a><br /><br /><br /><br />Phallocracy: "Juliette" by by Marquis de Sade, Austryn Wainhouse (Trans.)<br /><br /><br /><br />(Original Review, 1981-04-04)<br /><br /><br /><br />Histoire de Juliette ou les Prospérités du Vice (Story of Juliette or The Prosperities of Vice) by de Sade.<br /><br />Profoundly disturbing - not only in its depiction of cold-hearted indulgence, by way of a text nearly as long as War and Peace, in murder, rape, robbery and more horrors besides, but also in its capability to beguile and confuse readers of a feminist persuasion.<br /><br />Angela Carter fell for it: "[Sade's] great women [characters in Juliette], ... once they have tasted power, once they know how to use their sexuality as an instrument of aggression, they use it to extract vengeance for the humiliations they were forced to endure as the passive objects of the sexual energy of others ...”</span>

<div id="review_753304790" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/753304790" />
    <a title="Tamara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7411960-tamara"><img alt="Tamara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602612965p2/7411960.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/753304790?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tamara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tamara" href="/user/show/7411960-tamara">Tamara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 1499031432" href="/book/show/636035.Juliette">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer753304790" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15816117760806797004">Rarely do I write a review on a book I'm not finished with, but this warrants it, as I've taken a bit of heat for reading this in the first place.<br/>Firstly, this book is a tough read, due to the incredibly disturbing sexual violence. Before I hit 100 pages, I put it down, determined to never finish it. However, prevailed upon by Oscar Wilde ("“There is no such thing as a moral or an immoral book. Books are well written, or badly written. That is all"), I decided to give The Marquis another go, and</span>
  <span id="freeText15816117760806797004" style="display:none">Rarely do I write a review on a book I'm not finished with, but this warrants it, as I've taken a bit of heat for reading this in the first place.<br />Firstly, this book is a tough read, due to the incredibly disturbing sexual violence. Before I hit 100 pages, I put it down, determined to never finish it. However, prevailed upon by Oscar Wilde ("“There is no such thing as a moral or an immoral book. Books are well written, or badly written. That is all"), I decided to give The Marquis another go, and I am glad I did.<br />Juliette is better read with some insight into the life of The Marquis, which isn't quite what most historians would have you to believe. While he was a deviant, by most "normal" standards, he appears to be innocent of the majority of the villainy portrayed in his books. What he was, though, was a champion of the oppressed, which he obviously considered himself. He, though a member of the aristocracy, wasn't "one of them", and suffered a great deal at their hands, especially the hands of his mother in law. Karma come full circle. in later years, The Marquis himself refused to sign her death warrant, as he firmly disapproved of the death penalty. Ironic.<br />This book offers an insight into French history that no text book will ever give, and a clearer understanding of why the French Revolution was such a blood bath. The Marquis's obvious contempt, if not outright hatred, for the elite, the church, and the bankers make him perhaps one of the first to loudly oppose "the 1%" that is so often blasted today for, ironically again, similar abuses. I believe The Marquis de Sade to be a master of metaphor, albeit not ones I would have chosen. The man was clearly brilliant, and one hell of a writer. I also admire the fact that The Marquis had the courage of his conviction, to the point that he said, in a letter to his wife, "either kill me or accept me as I am, for may hell freeze over if I ever change". In my book, that's called chutzpah.<br />Perhaps my opinion will change when I finish the book, although I doubt it. This book is not a book of pornography, it is a book of philosophy. Anyone who reads this for the sex depicted in it should seek psychiatric help. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15816117760806797004" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_363137765" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/363137765" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer363137765" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2271746095902651272">I read about half of <i>Juliette</i> about 20 years ago. It's a long, repetitive book so please forgive me for abandoning it! Can I give four stars to a book that I abandoned? Four stars for a book that I've described as repetitive? Yes, I can...it's flawed but astonishing.<br/><br/>Although I'm sure that there are people somewhere who would consider de Sade a lightweight, I'm also sure that 99.99% of the population would consider it pretty intense. For example I remember getting to the point where de Sade had c</span>
  <span id="freeText2271746095902651272" style="display:none">I read about half of <i>Juliette</i> about 20 years ago. It's a long, repetitive book so please forgive me for abandoning it! Can I give four stars to a book that I abandoned? Four stars for a book that I've described as repetitive? Yes, I can...it's flawed but astonishing.<br /><br />Although I'm sure that there are people somewhere who would consider de Sade a lightweight, I'm also sure that 99.99% of the population would consider it pretty intense. For example I remember getting to the point where de Sade had concocted a scene where there was a row of women with babies on their shoulders, the children were then stabbed and the blood ran down the women's backs who defecated at this point so that a row of kneeling men ate the blood/faeces mixture. Knowing de Sade the men were probably masturbating and/or being sodomised at the same time - 'Good Grief!!!' I remember getting to this point of the book feeling desensitised and numb and decided to stop.<br /><br />It's the unrelenting monotony of de Sade's sexual visions and scenarios that had me in the end. They should be described as foul and depraved rather than as erotic as they focus so much on shit, piss, blood and vomit and the sex isn't kinky, it's extreme and painfully violent. I think I felt sick most of the time rather than aroused. Though if you're into pain and body fluids you'll probably find it titillating.<br /><br />Between the sexual perversions, when the libertines are relaxing and eating, there are usually passages in which the characters voice de Sade's political and philosophical views. These actually became more interesting than the monotonous sexual acts and de Sade comes across as a forerunner to Nietzsche - opposed to religion and in favour of man taking control of his own destiny.<br /><br />Everyone should read some Sade at some point as it's truly astonishing to read. You just need to remember that it is fiction, it is the product of a person's mind and not an historical record. This did not actually happen! After all people read murder-mysteries and crime thrillers but do not condone the criminal acts that are described. Presumably they realise that it's just a story.<br /><br />The Marquis is a very interesting historical figure and about the same time as I read <i>Juliette</i> I also read a biography by Maurice Lever which is thoroughly recommended.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2271746095902651272" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer189217747" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7583051028180930277">Juliette is to Justine what Ada is to Lolita: there are shades of the greater, less widely read novel in the lesser but more culturally known work. I can't say it much better than another reviewer more famous than me. If Sade is a religion, this is its bible. (And if you liked Justine, you may be sorry to hear she has a cameo in Juliette.)<br/><br/>Sade the philosopher is in force, and his treatises - like his other novels delivered by key characters - are interspersed with the sex and violence one expect</span>
  <span id="freeText7583051028180930277" style="display:none">Juliette is to Justine what Ada is to Lolita: there are shades of the greater, less widely read novel in the lesser but more culturally known work. I can't say it much better than another reviewer more famous than me. If Sade is a religion, this is its bible. (And if you liked Justine, you may be sorry to hear she has a cameo in Juliette.)<br /><br />Sade the philosopher is in force, and his treatises - like his other novels delivered by key characters - are interspersed with the sex and violence one expects. And yet...is it Sade who speaks through his canon's most libertine characters, or is he an ironist an order of magnitude more accomplished than Swift? I recommend a brief familiarity with his biography and political beliefs, and probably reading his Philosophy in the Bedroom, before reading Juliette. When a reader falls in stride with Sade, and any prudish shock wears off, it becomes easier to discern what's true to his idealistic vision of humanity, what's buffoonery, and what are prescient satirical riffs and exposés of the cultural idiosyncrasies he endured in eighteenth-century France. Otherwise Sade can appear to be a mass of contradictions. He respects the state and the institution of democracy yet rejects law. His characters expound on their superiority over other living things yet he writes brilliantly on the equality of animals and humans. And so on. <br /><br />In any case Juliette is a masterpiece of fiction with many concurrent undertones. Juliette is a feminist far before her time, a heroine and anti-heroine, a victim-turned-predator who rejects conventional sex roles and relationships (and perhaps one of literature's first unrepentant bisexuals). There will always be a question of whether Sade is pornographic, but there can never be a question of his value to literature. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7583051028180930277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_40211788" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40211788" />
    <a title="Jesse" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1807737-jesse"><img alt="Jesse" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598868134p2/1807737.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/40211788?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jesse" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jesse" href="/user/show/1807737-jesse">Jesse</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1807737-jesse?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40211788" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3596555210508208322">Even though I am only almost through with Juliette, I feel as though I have managed to grasp the essence of the book already and can, therefore, give my thoughts on it. This is the Sadeian novel at its most perfected. It is the Don Quixote of Sade's novels, in other words. <br/>Now, with that stated, I would like to address some complaints I've seen other members mention in regards to this book. <br/>Is it boring? Well, it wasn't to me. Sade has never bored me. Some readers might, of course be bored to te</span>
  <span id="freeText3596555210508208322" style="display:none">Even though I am only almost through with Juliette, I feel as though I have managed to grasp the essence of the book already and can, therefore, give my thoughts on it. This is the Sadeian novel at its most perfected. It is the Don Quixote of Sade's novels, in other words. <br />Now, with that stated, I would like to address some complaints I've seen other members mention in regards to this book. <br />Is it boring? Well, it wasn't to me. Sade has never bored me. Some readers might, of course be bored to tears by this huge novel but I don't think that a book (particularly Sade's novels) is by nature boring. It all depends on whether or not the reader found the subject matter and general tone of the novel interesting. Therefore, it is the reader who makes a text 'boring' not the text alone. And I, as a reader, did not find any of this book's contents to be boring. <br />Is it repetitive? Sade's texts by their very nature are repetitive. Most of the scenes follow a particular schematic: discourse, orgy, discourse, orgy, etc. Now, whereas many readers might suggest that each discourse is merely a repetition of the others, I found each discourse to build upon the last in much the same way that the orgies do. The orgies and discourses go a step further each time until, finally, all hell breaks loose and the discourse becomes loftier in order to justify more grotesque horrors. <br />Is it "disgusting, depraved, or evil"? I won't respond to this claim because whoever comes to this conclusion is either, first of all, acting shocked in order to make themselves appear more innocent or, second of all, reading a copy that doesn't mention the author's name or any summary of the text on the cover because, after all, people expecting light-hearted romantic romps don't pick up books by Sade. <br /> Now, since I'm not qualified to go into a literary criticism of this work, I would suggest reading "Sade: A Sudden Abyss" (covers the entirety of Sade's works) and "Writing the Orgy: Power and Parody in Sade" (covers Juliette exclusively). <br /> An absolutely stunning work. Maybe I'm weird but I was very satisfied with its tremendous length and, even after 1200 pages, was sad to see it end. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3596555210508208322" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17383913463029250658"><br/>Julliette, de Sade<br/>The Fortunes of Vice<br/><br/>When I was a young man consumed by curiosity, I came across a wretch known as de Sade.   <span id="freeText17383913463029250658" style="display:none"><br />Julliette, de Sade<br />The Fortunes of Vice<br /><br />When I was a young man consumed by curiosity, I came across a wretch known as de Sade. His extensive writings were radically immoral, and later endorsed by the left wing French philosophers Bataille and Foucault. He created a nausea hard to ignore. His descriptions of rape and crushing babies under his boots haunts me to this day.<br /><br />Certainly de Sade included popes and politicians within his broad denunciation of human worth. A misogynist, creature of kingdom and empire, unremorseful and disenfranchised, he considered it his birthright to abuse everyone he was able. He may hold a key to understanding the recent rise of popularism, criticized but little understood.<br /><br />Nobility once subjected those under their control. Not much has changed through the millennia that have passed. Now power comes from a bank account, not only from the barrel of a gun. When wealth was seized and criminals imprisoned in the Bastille, justice had been served. Until a new Napoleon rose from the ranks to save us from them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17383913463029250658" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_133139719" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133139719" />
    <a title="Jonesy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4286942-jonesy"><img alt="Jonesy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332545659p2/4286942.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/133139719?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonesy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonesy" href="/user/show/4286942-jonesy">Jonesy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 1499031432" href="/book/show/636035.Juliette">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133139719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7220446828058860649">I started regretting not making more of a spectacle out of the commentary for this book; I had wanted to do more Dracula: the Un-Dead style commentary to alleviate my pain incurred from reading this thing and creating something chuckle worthy for posterity's sake. At first I thought this was due to laziness on my part, but upon further reflection, I think I have found the problem.<br/><br/>Thee book is not only bad, it is repetitive in its badness.<br/><br/>Honestly, there's not much to say beyond the following pat</span>
  <span id="freeText7220446828058860649" style="display:none">I started regretting not making more of a spectacle out of the commentary for this book; I had wanted to do more Dracula: the Un-Dead style commentary to alleviate my pain incurred from reading this thing and creating something chuckle worthy for posterity's sake. At first I thought this was due to laziness on my part, but upon further reflection, I think I have found the problem.<br /><br />Thee book is not only bad, it is repetitive in its badness.<br /><br />Honestly, there's not much to say beyond the following pattern:<br /><br />1.) Ugh, an overwrought and boring (albeit sometimes creative) sex scene which is either physically impossible/gory to the point of either being gag-worthy or so cartoonish it can't faze an ADD kitten/replete with statements about how each successive character possesses the most perfect ass/any combination of the above. Can't wank to this since the book is so heavy. That and I am a sane human being.<br /><br />2.) Yawn, an overwrought and fallacious argument that keeps restating the same blatant contradictions and is rested on faulty at best premises. Socrates' proof of the doctrine of recollection by assuming an immortal soul and using that conclusion to prove the soul is immortal is starting to look really good now.<br /><br />3.) Oh wow, the story is now actually making me miss the sex and the philosophy. Kill me.<br /><br />4.) Rinse and repeat.<br /><br />5.)???<br /><br />6.)No profit.<br /><br /><br />I think I am correct in stating that this would have been dull as dishwater to post with any notable frequency. At least Stoker and Holt came up with something that was creatively stupid and left itself open to a variety of commentary, but not here. The cherry on the top of the sundae is that this went on for almost 1200 pages; unlike the other volumes of this ilk, there are no articles by other writers or other works of de Sade; the entire book is nothing but a giant trollfic, complete with at least one scene where the plot stops for the characters to review the books in a room and talk about what mean poopie heads the (presumably real)writers are and an overlong life story of a pointless character.<br /><br />There are rare bursts of insanity throughout the whole thing that are creative in their own way, however; at one point, a ludicrous and messed up albeit otherwise grounded in reality historical smut romp takes unsignaled left turns into fantasy with scenes involving giants that are so well hung that the act of sex kills their partner and a sorceress who has a potion that turns skeletons into well hung men.<br /><br />At one point, the protagonist, who in an earlier scene has been double penetrated by penises measuring about 9 inches in circumference and 13 inches in length with no problem, is presented with a penis that is 8 inches in circumference and a foot in length; upon sight of this, she starts complaining that it is so big it will kill her. Let's set aside the unlikely measurements and the dubious idea of a woman being able to participate in such a double penetration without suffering internal bleeding, let's even forget *everything* else wrong with this book and give our sole focus to this little problem.<br /><br />*ahem*<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />WHAT THE FRACKING HELL???????????????!!!!!!!!!!!<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />8 inches&lt;9 inches.<br /><br />12 inches&lt;13 inches.<br /><br />1 penis&lt;2 penises.<br /><br />This is basic math that a Kindergarten student could have done!*<br /><br />In no possible way should this hurt her, yet one bumtupping(*) later her fundament(*) is bleeding as if she had been trying to shove a copy of the manuscript in there. Unfortunately for me, this clearly did not happen, as the book was successfully published.<br /><br />The whole book continues this way, plodding along with no semblance of plot beyond the above listed routine. Finally, as the book drew to a close, I reflected how it would feel to be free of the author's smug footnotes telling the ladies to pay particular attention to this bit of bad argument, cite to alleged facts that have no discoverable notation linking them to the bibliography or giving page references, or (no joke) indicating that the book originally contained a recipe for a deadly poison that has been edited out of this release, and it felt sweet. I didn't even let my rage break at the realization that Justine's death breaks continuity with Justine, the book's sister novel. It was only when I realized that de Sade's creation was starting to break the fourth wall in the last two pages that I felt panic set in, compelling me to quickly finish and slam the book shut lest it cause a dimensional rift to open and let the insanity spill forth into our world.<br /><br />All in all, this book sucks. I am aware that similar problems were present to a lesser degree in the last 2 books I read, but the sheer magnitude of the bad stuff this time around and the ponderous length of the book make it intolerable. Do not read for any reason.<br /><br />*This is not an endorsement to use the Marquis de Sade's Juliette as a tool for teaching math to anyone of any age, ethnicity, race, national origin, creed, orientation, or any other class of self aware entities I have left out in my sleep deprived state of mind. Anyone who actually needed to read this to know that should remember that it is down the street, not across the road.**<br /><br />(*)Actual language used by the author. While it may have been commonplace at the time, any sense of steaminess that may have been induced by this language is killed by the characters using it in long unbroken sentences indicating they are having tea rather than nookie.<br /><br />**The writer of this review does not wish to drive anyone to suicide through this comment. Anyone who actually needed to read this to know that should send an email to freedesadebookforidiot@gmail.com and see what prize you have won.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7220446828058860649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_537245420" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/537245420" />
    <a title="Ihsan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/645265-ihsan"><img alt="Ihsan" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/537245420?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ihsan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ihsan" href="/user/show/645265-ihsan">Ihsan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer537245420" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4252893741685866743">If this was a better novel it would be one of the most notorious books ever written. Instead its author is notorious while his book is little-read.<br/><br/>The Marquis de Sade demonstrated powers of characterization and narrative in some his other works, but "Juliette" is a 1200-page slab of pornographic fantasy with philosophic pretensions. Like most wank fodder it is insanely repetitive: Juliette meets someone who shares her exact tastes; this figure gives an endless lecture of self-justification dress</span>
  <span id="freeText4252893741685866743" style="display:none">If this was a better novel it would be one of the most notorious books ever written. Instead its author is notorious while his book is little-read.<br /><br />The Marquis de Sade demonstrated powers of characterization and narrative in some his other works, but "Juliette" is a 1200-page slab of pornographic fantasy with philosophic pretensions. Like most wank fodder it is insanely repetitive: Juliette meets someone who shares her exact tastes; this figure gives an endless lecture of self-justification dressed up as philosophy; they have an orgy wherein many disposable partners are raped, tortured, killed. Then the cycle restarts over and over again. The violence of the orgies builds, but they're repeated so endlessly (alongside their prefatory lectures) that the reader is desensitized. If the book were only 300 pages long, it would have the concentrated power of snake venom. <br /><br />"Juliette" even fails as pornography—-de Sade is more interested in describing sexual positions, via interminably detailed tableaux, than in describing the sensations of sex. But readers will get more than enough philosophical justification for the acts of sexual violence. De Sade’s basic philosophy is “I’m an asshole and I should be allowed to do whatever I want.” Why? Because such impulses are inspired by Nature--and Nature even approves of murder, because it frees up space for new beings! Despite being a committed atheist (many of his arguments are stolen from Baron D’Holbach), de Sade makes a Goddess out of Nature—-she endlessly “desires” whatever crime he feels like committing. What is more, mankind is claimed to have been originally solitary--a scientifically false idea derived from Rousseau. Like true sociopaths, de Sade's characters dismiss empathy and altruism as mere expressions of self-interest. How chagrined he would be to know the softer emotions he despised were shared by dolphins, elephants, chimpanzees, and other highly-intelligent Social animals! Such feelings and sociability are entirely natural, whereas de Sade’s characters are freaks, and their creator's view of nature is bunk. <br /><br />De Sade needs nature’s backing to justify writing outrageous acts of cruelty, comprised of tortures that would make a concentration camp attendant proud. The book is in love with the idea of an egoistic self that uses others in any way it likes and destroys them to gratify its power. Hence the literal disposability of Juliette’s sex-victims—-thousands die to give her orgasms. None of her victims ever think to fight back or resist their sexual degradation and destruction--that would spoil the fantasy as much as giving the reader a chance to get to know Juliette's victims.<br /><br />If this is such a failure of a novel, why do I give it three stars rather than none? Because de Sade writes with such evil energy; because his imagination is so fertile is devising the worst for mankind; because he goes to such Olympian lengths to invert the moral values of western civilization, even if his philosophy is ultimately spurious. Spend enough time with this book and you will feel de Sade working on your brain, daring you to indulge your worst self. <br /><br />De Sade was only too convincing in showing the delight in making other suffer. In one of hundreds of sexual tortures, Juliette nibbles a boy’s penis until it’s almost severed. "Where did I read this before?" I thought, before remembering it was from an account of the mass-murder spree of Dean Corll and his accomplices, who sexually tortured and murdered nearly 30 teenaged boys in the 1970s--all for reasons as purely sadistic as de Sade’s. I shuddered at realizing the kinship in mentality. "Juliette" presages the atrocities of our own age; it still shocks and haunts. <br /><br />A final note: the translation, by Austryn Wainhouse, is adept but irritating. He overuses fustian expressions like “‘tis” and has obnoxious tics like always referring to semen as “fuck” or employing pointlessly recondite words like “bardash” and “fustigate.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="4252893741685866743" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_504801601" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/504801601" />
    <a title="Squire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15986821-squire"><img alt="Squire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484920763p2/15986821.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/504801601?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Squire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Squire" href="/user/show/15986821-squire">Squire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15986821-squire?shelf=tomes-doorstops-challenge-2017">tomes-doorstops-challenge-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15986821-squire?shelf=classics-challenge-2017">classics-challenge-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer504801601" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1547297602149143417">This book is a lot of things. It's a well-written and imaginative masterpiece of cold, brutal philosophy; it's a cornucopia of jaw-dropping, though ultimately numbing, horror; it's an encyclopedia of repetitive, boring sexual escapades (different sex, same sex, group sex, incest, beastiality, necrophilia, bum-stuffing, mard-munching, golden showers: pretty much all fetish bases covered)--in short, it's an experience that is not for the faint-hearted.<br/><br/>There's a passion and a joy to all 1205 pages </span>
  <span id="freeText1547297602149143417" style="display:none">This book is a lot of things. It's a well-written and imaginative masterpiece of cold, brutal philosophy; it's a cornucopia of jaw-dropping, though ultimately numbing, horror; it's an encyclopedia of repetitive, boring sexual escapades (different sex, same sex, group sex, incest, beastiality, necrophilia, bum-stuffing, mard-munching, golden showers: pretty much all fetish bases covered)--in short, it's an experience that is not for the faint-hearted.<br /><br />There's a passion and a joy to all 1205 pages that propels the story and makes even the long stretches of philosophy fun to read. Sade can playful with his language and clearly loves telling obscene stories. But his natural tendency to direct a stage version of his orgies trips him up as he blocks the various "scenes" of libertinage in the book before they begin, rendering them artificial and uninteresting after a time; however, every once in a while, when he has only two actors (usually two women), his pornography becomes quite titillating. That usually only lasts a paragraph or two, but it does provide a refreshing break from the drudgery of the orgies. And while the crimes perpetrated by the characters start out as shocking, they become less so as the the page count amasses and the crimes become more outrageous. I became desensitized to the horrors of <i>Juliette</i> by the end of Part Four (around page 800).<br /><br />My favorite part of the book was the novella-length story told by the Italian highwayman who captures Juliette. Fortunately, it began on page 815 and gave me my second wind with this book.<br /><br />I thought that after reading <i>Justine, or Good Conduct Well Chastised</i> that the Marquis detested women. But it turns out that he only detests women who are virtuous. Those free of social bondages and of a libertine and criminal bent he admires. So add the Marquis to your list of feminist authors.<br /><br />I won't recommend this book to anyone. Honestly, if you are of this bent or are curious as to what Sade is really about (outside reputation and the movies), you'll find your way here on your own. I was of the curious who did and I have fully sated that curiosity. I'm done with him. So I now consign the Marquis to the oblivion of eternity he so desired and a dusty book shelf in my basement. I at least now know the truth behind the legend.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1547297602149143417" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_210464015" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/210464015" />
    <a title="Darius Whiteplume" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6327966-darius-whiteplume"><img alt="Darius Whiteplume" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316394915p2/6327966.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/210464015?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darius Whiteplume" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darius Whiteplume" href="/user/show/6327966-darius-whiteplume">Darius Whiteplume</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer210464015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16879746449245538497">In many ways this is Sade's magnum opus. It also contains more actual eroticism than his other works, which tend to be mere torture porn. Juliette has actual relationships and is not invulnerable to the nagging humanity that plagues all classic villains. She can be swayed by her emotion, and while evil, is not</span>

<div id="review_412202023" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/412202023" />
    <a title="Julia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11139775-julia"><img alt="Julia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1342837825p2/11139775.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/412202023?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julia" href="/user/show/11139775-julia">Julia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11139775-julia?shelf=written-reviews">written-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer412202023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9688930109439040819">I read/owned this book many years ago, and eventually sold the book to a used bookstore, because I could not see myself reading it for a second time. I confess that I only picked this de Sade book in particular (I was curious about de Sade anyway) because of the title character. Since my name is so awesome (har har), I gravitate toward characters who share it with me.<br/><br/>I did like the book, and it did open my eyes to new things... but damn, if this book is not disturbing. The sex is kinky every tim</span>
  <span id="freeText9688930109439040819" style="display:none">I read/owned this book many years ago, and eventually sold the book to a used bookstore, because I could not see myself reading it for a second time. I confess that I only picked this de Sade book in particular (I was curious about de Sade anyway) because of the title character. Since my name is so awesome (har har), I gravitate toward characters who share it with me.<br /><br />I did like the book, and it did open my eyes to new things... but damn, if this book is not disturbing. The sex is kinky every time, but I found myself nodding off and skimming when any of the characters started ranting about something they didn't like.<br /><br />Essentially, this book (well all of them, really) was a way for de Sade to talk about the things he hated the most. Which is quite a bit - virginity, marriage, the government, the clergy, anything vanilla, pregnancy, babies, vaginal sex (because anal sex is so much better), etc. Nothing is untouched, the list goes on and on. There are several "lectures" given by several different characters, and I think the funniest are the ones about virginity and marriage given by priests and nuns who are all having sex with each other. Virginity must be banished and no one should ever get married. The irony is hilarious. Also, murder is a frequent side effect of everything, no consequences anywhere (why would there be consequences, justice as we know it does not exist anywhere in this story).<br /><br />Sex in this book includes: the taking of everyone's virginity in the book (this is not very far off the mark), sex with clergy, beating, spanking, sex using torture objects, blood play, anal sex, pedophilia, heterosexuality, homosexuality, orgies (sex with just two people involved in this book is actually very rare), sex involving fecal play, rape, incest, being sexed until someone dies (usually a woman), having sex with dead people. The only thing I don't remember occurring is bestiality, but if I read it again, I would not be surprised to find it buried in there somewhere. Juliette gets pregnant with her father's baby, has an abortion, and talks about how wonderful it was because it made her so much slimmer and more fit than she was pre-pregnancy. When she does eventually give birth to a child, she uses her for sexual favors at an older age, but otherwise pretends that her daughter doesn't exist. I'm pretty sure she ends up killing her father, and the last scene in the book involves Juliette facilitating her sister Justine's death, in a sexual way, of course (Justine stars in another de Sade book).<br /><br />The "point" of the book is that morality, religion, law, etc., and other similar concepts don't mean anything, and you should find and take the pleasure you need, no matter who gets hurt. This is not a book for someone with a weak stomach. And it is NOT a romance novel. I don't EVER support censorship for any reason, I support free will and ability to judge ideas with your own mind; but this book was banned for good reasons. Some people would not be able to handle this. If the movie "Quills" bothered you in any capacity (a story based on Marquis de Sade's imprisonment) DO NOT READ THIS.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9688930109439040819" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3360505640" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3360505640" />
    <a title="Brian O&#39;Connell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24209733-brian-o-connell"><img alt="Brian O&#39;Connell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589153542p2/24209733.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3360505640?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian O&#39;Connell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian O&#39;Connell" href="/user/show/24209733-brian-o-connell">Brian O&#39;Connell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24209733-brian-o-connell?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24209733-brian-o-connell?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24209733-brian-o-connell?shelf=queer">queer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24209733-brian-o-connell?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24209733-brian-o-connell?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24209733-brian-o-connell?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24209733-brian-o-connell?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24209733-brian-o-connell?shelf=transgressive">transgressive</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3360505640" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer705226223173756130"><blockquote>"'All the persons, all the creatures about you shall look to you like so many victims destiny has led up in fetters to sate your heart's perversity. No more duties, no more hampering ties, no more obstacles to impede you, they'll all vanish in a trice, dissolved by the vehemence of your desires. No longer from the depths of your soul shall any voice speak reproachfully, hoping to impair your vigor and rob you of joy. Nevermore shall prejudice militate against your happiness, wisdom shall abolish</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText705226223173756130" style="display:none"><blockquote>"'All the persons, all the creatures about you shall look to you like so many victims destiny has led up in fetters to sate your heart's perversity. No more duties, no more hampering ties, no more obstacles to impede you, they'll all vanish in a trice, dissolved by the vehemence of your desires. No longer from the depths of your soul shall any voice speak reproachfully, hoping to impair your vigor and rob you of joy. Nevermore shall prejudice militate against your happiness, wisdom shall abolish every check, and with even stride you shall walk along a pathway strewn thick with flowers, till finally you accede to perversity's ultimate excesses.'" (pgs. 18—19)</blockquote><br /><i>Juliette</i> is the latest of Sade's libertine novels. Released from 1797 to 1801 as the latter six volumes of <i>La Nouvelle Justine</i> (the first four volumes of which no translator has been brave enough to undertake yet—somebody! Please!), it was written and published in his late fifties, and represented his last opportunity to put out such a book. Napoleon ordered his final imprisonment on account of this text ("the most abominable book ever engendered by the most depraved imagination": what a blurb!), and most of his further writings have been lost or destroyed. He never stopped working, however: at the time of his death he was in the early stages of <i>The Days of Florbelle</i>, another planned ten-volume opus that promised to be a further escalation (if such a thing is possible) of his frightening fantasies. But his son had this burnt and few traces survive.<br /><br />So <i>Juliette</i> is the last of his major works we have. It is also the most mature, in that it represents the absolute culmination of a worldview that had been steadily developing for over a decade. This outlook had been born in the Bastille and found its most distressing expression with <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29776923.The_120_Days_of_Sodom" title="The 120 Days of Sodom by Marquis de Sade" rel="nofollow noopener">The 120 Days of Sodom</a>
</i>, a book which, while it contains brief philosophical discourses between its four monstrous protagonists, is almost entirely consumed by systematic accounts of sexual perversion, and owing to its draft status devolves by its end into a numbered list of unspeakable tortures. (Some scholars assume <i>Juliette</i> was Sade's attempt to recapture something of this work's hallucinatory fervor, and while it doesn't quite rise to the earlier text's coprophiliac excess and nightmarish precision, it certainly matches it—perhaps even exceeds it—in body count.) As his work evolved, the balance between libertine philosophy and sadistic sexual violence became more even. The form of <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/152113.Philosophy_in_the_Boudoir" title="Philosophy in the Boudoir by Marquis de Sade" rel="nofollow noopener">Philosophy in the Boudoir</a>
</i> was an overt parody of philosophical dialogue, alternating with regularity between pornographic orgies and lengthy dissertations. The 1791 version of <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16182384.Justine__or_the_Misfortunes_of_Virtue" title="Justine, or the Misfortunes of Virtue by Marquis de Sade" rel="nofollow noopener">Justine, or the Misfortunes of Virtue</a>
</i> took the shape of a <i>conte moral</i>, a kind of fable-like "demonstration" of this philosophy's principles in action. But <i>La Nouvelle Justine</i>, of which <i>Juliette</i> consumes the greater part, is an epic. This is most immediately apparent in its length: the Grove Press edition of <i>Juliette</i> is almost 1,200 pages long. Moreover, like all of Sade's books, these pages are for the most part incredibly repetitive. When Juliette and her vicious compatriots aren't fucking, traveling, or plotting some dastardly scheme, they are talking: about love, death, money, society, history, government, the sexes, just about everything one could have conceived of in Sade's time. But wherever Juliette roams, whomever she speaks to, and whatever they talk about, the conclusions are always the same: the denial of belief both in a deity and in any sort of common bond between human beings, the absoluteness of self-interest, the primacy of pleasure-seeking, and the supremacy of vice and crime over virtue and justice. These are all arguments that Sade's characters have made before, and in the same way: the cultural relativism, the ceaseless execration of religion, the constant evocation of Nature, etc. But never before have they been made in such fervent detail. The first part of <i>Juliette</i>, wherein she leaps from nunnery to brothel to the bed of her parents' murderer, is almost entirely taken up by these speeches; they grow somewhat less constant over the rest of the book, but no less vehement. Every one of these characters has something to say on the matter—often quite a lot to say. The Pope's defense of murder, the culminating movement of Part Four, lasts over thirty pages. At some point, buried both by the aggressive philosophical disquisitions and the enormous heap of bodies slain by the libertines, one has to ask: who would really devote so much time, so many pages, indeed, so much of their freedom, to argue at such great length what essentially amounts to a justification for all evils?<br /><br /><blockquote>"'The stocks, the pillory, the scaffold itself would for me be a privilege, the throne of delight, upon it I'd cry death defiance, and discharge in the pleasure of perishing the victim of my crimes and over the idea that in the future my name would be a byword for evil, at whose mere mention generations of men would tremble.'" (pgs. 663—664)</blockquote><br />Sade is, of course, a notoriously ambiguous and hard-to-pin-down figure, one who eludes most clear-cut interpretations. Is he a satirist? The "great poet of the anarchy of power", as Pasolini called him? (Oh, by the way: go watch <i>Salò</i>.) A sick, perverted pornographer? A libertarian? A psychopathic misogynist? Even, as some have suggested, a <a href="https://www.academia.edu/37993109/Sade_Enlightenment_Holocaust" rel="nofollow noopener">forebear to totalitarian atrocity</a>?<br /><br />Well, I don't pretend to have the answers here. But for the purposes of reading and writing about him, I'll generally tend to side with Simone de Beauvoir's characteristically insightful assessment of the man in her seminal essay "Must We Burn Sade?", which, though it draws on now-dated biographical sources and makes some spurious claims about his own sexuality, helped me formulate a clearer understanding of the relationship between him and his art. It's the story of a man who, entering into adulthood, "made the brutal discovery that there was no conciliation possible between his social existence and his private pleasures": that is, that his sexual predilections would not be tolerated by his class or in his time. And it is worth noting that, aside from the rape of Rose Keller (the only overtly "sadistic" sexual act we know he committed) and the likely orgies with underage youths at La Coste, his sexual idiosyncrasies for the most part would not be considered abnormal or even hugely reprehensible today. (Indeed, remarks Beauvoir, "[m]any libertines of the period indulged with impunity in orgies even worse.") But his punishment with imprisonment obviously failed to quiet the dark stirrings of his fantasies, as <i>The 120 Days</i> proves. Indeed, by Sade's own admission (from a letter quoted on page 20 of Annie Le Brun's <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/497007.Sade_A_Sudden_Abyss" title="Sade A Sudden Abyss by Annie Le Brun" rel="nofollow noopener">Sade: A Sudden Abyss</a>
</i>), they were only intensified: "you all think you have accomplished a miracle, I would bet on it, in reducing me to an appalling state of abstinence in regard to <i>carnal sinning</i>. Well, you are mistaken: you have merely fired my imagination...When one overheats the pot, you know quite well it must boil over." Beauvoir therefore analyzes the transgressive philosophy found in Sade's libertine fiction as "a challenge to society", an expression of open rebellion on the world that incarcerated him for the way he was made. This is not to dub Sade as a champion of free speech (one of the more obnoxiously simple popular views on him), nor is it to write him off as a twisted murder-porn scribe. It's foolish to believe Sade was sexually aroused by every perversion he accounted for in his books. His own sexuality is in there, of course, but it's in engaged in a complex interplay with his anger. His books are, as the writer <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/765702.Gemma_Files" title="Gemma Files" rel="nofollow noopener">Gemma Files</a> once said to me, "a wrestling match between his genuine fetishes and his anarchic urge to destroy or undermine all structures of moral authority".<br /><br />Now, this isn't really a very scholarly or academic view, but I'm personally inclined to agree with fellow Goodreads reviewer Kezia's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/189217747?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">take</a> on the experience of reading Sade's work. "When a reader falls in stride with Sade," she writes, "and any prudish shock wears off, it becomes easier to discern what's true to his idealistic vision of humanity, what's buffoonery, and what are prescient satirical riffs and exposés of the cultural idiosyncrasies he endured in eighteenth-century France." Did Sade really support the mass murder of all Catholics, the burning of orphanages and poorhouses, the engineering of devastating famines and epidemics to decimate the population? It is hard to believe that a man so attuned to the hypocrisies of the powerful and (importantly) so opposed to the consuming violence of the Terror, to which he refused to sacrifice even his much-hated in-laws, sincerely held such views. That said, it is clear that no-one could linger for so long on fantasies of torturing young children, on arguing so doggedly against any natural camaraderie between human beings, with purely satirical intentions. But, as aforesaid, this is half the challenge of reading Sade: figuring out the author's position on violence. By the end, if you're like me, you still won't have come to a conclusion. So we'll put the question aside for now.<br /><br /><blockquote>"The Bolognese nun possesses the art of cunt-sucking in a higher degree than any other female on the European continent..." (pg. 573)</blockquote><br /><i>Juliette</i> is funny. It is, of course, also extremely horrifying and disgusting, but Sade's sense of humor and general madness can make for an amusingly outrageous read. You'll encounter some real zingers across the bloodshed, such as page 630's assertion that "Man is weak. The pious are weaker than most, especially when you offer them boys. Seldom sufficiently stressed, often not even realized, there exists a powerful analogy between believers in God and buggers."<br /><br />I think what often goes uncommented-on about Sade's libertine fiction is that the undergirding drive of his narratives really is the pleasure principle. In his essay "<a href="https://www.splicetoday.com/writing/reading-juliette-by-the-marquis-de-sade" rel="nofollow noopener">Reading <i>Juliette</i></a>", which provides certain insights that most Sadean critics bypass completely, Joseph Kugelmass argues that "Sade is a towering figure in any literary canon founded upon joy, for reasons at once enlightening and complex." This is at least partially because there is no filler in Sade: everything that happens on a given page, be it philosophy, pornography, violence, or narrative, is written to please him at that moment. "<i>Juliette</i> has no other content besides pleasure," writes Kugelmass. "If de Sade includes something, you better believe he thinks it's delicious." Its repetitiveness also ensures that, as Kugelmass suggests, it is "a fractal": you could read five pages, two hundred, or the whole damn thing, and each time you would take away largely the same thing. This lends it both a potentially tedious monotony and a dizzying obsessiveness, like you're seeing into the deepest recesses of someone's mind. But either way, it's undeniable that Sade delivers what his readers expect. If you share my morbid curiosity, then you'll be amply rewarded.<br /><br />So yes: while <i>Juliette</i> is revolting, brutal, and exhausting, it is also (if you're actually interested in engaging with this material) imaginative, exhilarating, and kind of amazing. Juliette traverses a whole world of spectacular darkness and amorality. She encounters all manner of remarkable, frightening figures: the murderous billionaire Saint-Fond, who decapitates his victims as he fucks them and has conceived of a (unusually, for Sade) quasi-religious metaphysics of evil; the gigantic cannibal Muscovite Minski, "Hermit of the Apennines", whose cock is so massive it mortally wounds its recipients and whose murderous urges are so extreme that not even Juliette dares to stick around with him for long; the highwayman Bristatesta, who briefly takes up the mantle of narrator in Part Five to relate his own hallucinatory, horrifying life story; the aristocratic Cordelli, whose tortures are so punishingly awful they distressed me even after five volumes of the same; and, perhaps my favorite, the misandrist Clairwil, who easily gets the best lines and forms a particularly close, <i>almost</i> outright romantic bond with Juliette. We even get to see the final fate of Justine, even harsher than in the earlier version. This is a novel that begins with proto-nunsploitation orgies and ends with the most appalling wedding scene in literature. I could not have plowed through this doorstop of a book if I didn't find it at least somewhat entertaining, and Sade's imagination, though certainly limited in its interests and aspirations, is so rich, expansive, and addictive that, by the time I turned the last page, I was sorry to see it go.<br /><br /><blockquote>"'There are terrible truths to bare in this world, and ungrateful, nay, distressing is the mission of those who from love of mankind are forced to reveal them.'" (pg. 632)</blockquote><br />I don't know who I'm writing this review for. No-one is going to read Sade if they don't want to. Those who have are either not keen to revisit him or already tuned in to what I'm saying. And it's not like the disjointed ramblings I've presented above really amount to much: messes of citations and confused impressions, a review that conveys some sort of "response" to <i>Juliette</i> but not the real <i>sense</i> of it. (I didn't even get to regurgitate the interesting points about Sadean language, the way his deliberately non-descriptive phrasing makes the reader co-author, complicit.)<br /><br />Perhaps I'm writing it for myself, because I honestly can't say exactly what it is that drove me to read so much of his work, what it is that I get out of his books. I can offer the usual explanations—that they're fascinating looks into man's darkest impulses, that they provide a completely alternative worldview, that they're historically or ethically important, that they're valuable statements against censorship, etc., etc.—but they wouldn't really suffice. At the core, there really is something deeply mysterious and perhaps unknowable that nobody has really been able to define. That core might repel you, or exert fascination on you, but there's no denying its power or uniqueness. Perhaps it's the man himself who said it best. On page 791 one of his characters suggests "'there is always something interesting and sublime about the violent aspects of Nature.'" I'd be being hypocritical if I said that I didn't agree.</span>
  <a data-text-id="705226223173756130" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13920020232361107885">Awesome! Possibly Sade's best book, closely followed by "Philosophy in the Bedroom". <br/>While the sex scenes are not as crude as in the "120 Days", there's still enough of Sade's wonderful philosophy to keep the reader interested.   <span id="freeText13920020232361107885" style="display:none">Awesome! Possibly Sade's best book, closely followed by "Philosophy in the Bedroom". <br />While the sex scenes are not as crude as in the "120 Days", there's still enough of Sade's wonderful philosophy to keep the reader interested. And the sex scenes are enjoyable, since they are not that extreme.<br />Sade does an excellent job of describing human nature. The book makes any reader that still considers human nature to be, in essence, good, wake up and face reality. And while by any standard Sade's characters would qualify as sociopaths or psychopaths, human nature is indeed selfish and cruel to a considerable degree.<br />The author also considers humanity's place in the Universe. We are as unimportant to nature as flies are and it is best to remember that. In essence, killing a human being is no more a crime than killing a roach. Still, the book is written as an exercise of pushing the boundaries and it is more than clear that the empathy evolution endowed us with prevents most of us from comitting acts as those described in Sade's book (except for psychopaths, of course). <br />I do not consider that this is a moralistic book at its core. As in, look what happens if all moral rules are foresaken and pleasure is the only god. No. Those who say so show, in my opinion, that they don't understand Sade at all. Then again, Sade himself knew that not everybody would understand his books. </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer18419953186311539380">While deciding to read this book I was slightly apprehensive whether it would be solely filled with obscenities or it would legitimately transmit a different take on life and society's dogmas. So far, I have been pleasantly surprised.<br/><br/>I have found Juliette to be an extensive reflection on the matter of religion, monogamy and other subjects of the sorts. Yes, it does have various explicit passages but those are purely a way of demonstrating the alternative mind of Marquis de Sade. Demonstratively,</span>
  <span id="freeText18419953186311539380" style="display:none">While deciding to read this book I was slightly apprehensive whether it would be solely filled with obscenities or it would legitimately transmit a different take on life and society's dogmas. So far, I have been pleasantly surprised.<br /><br />I have found Juliette to be an extensive reflection on the matter of religion, monogamy and other subjects of the sorts. Yes, it does have various explicit passages but those are purely a way of demonstrating the alternative mind of Marquis de Sade. Demonstratively, one of the characters of the book states: "There's more to it than just experiencing sensations, they must also be analyzed. Sometimes it is as pleasant to discuss as to undergo them; and when one has reached the limit of one's physical means, one may then exploit one's intellect".<br /><br />The book presents an eloquent language and proves the author not as an obscene man but as an well informed, lived and educated person.<br /><br />I strongly recommend this book to anyone of a mature mind who is interested in familiarizing himself with a divergent perspective on life, sexuality and human interactions.</span>

<div id="review_175708574" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175708574" />

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1254655884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8575514594638743000">I believe that this is one of the best critics to society that I've read. I did't particularly like the parts of abuse, shit eating, piss drinking and human flesh eating, and even after the first half I still got disgusted, and until the end I was disgusted.<br/>But the society is still as corrupt as it was back in Sade' days.<br/>All religions are discriminatory towards womans.<br/>The governments prefer their pleople ignorant as extremly obedient to their whishes.<br/>Powerfull and rich people always manage to ge</span>
  <span id="freeText8575514594638743000" style="display:none">I believe that this is one of the best critics to society that I've read. I did't particularly like the parts of abuse, shit eating, piss drinking and human flesh eating, and even after the first half I still got disgusted, and until the end I was disgusted.<br />But the society is still as corrupt as it was back in Sade' days.<br />All religions are discriminatory towards womans.<br />The governments prefer their pleople ignorant as extremly obedient to their whishes.<br />Powerfull and rich people always manage to get impune to their crimes.<br />Women are still considered as an object in many places of our earth.<br />Etc... <br />I believe that the way to better criticize the society, was to make some eat shit and always be sodomized as a little lamb, or as a preverted crazy person. He was brilliant, but sometimes so terrifying that it was ridiculous, and impossible to be true (30cm long and 20cm in diameter of pennis).<br />This isn't a book for everyone. I confess that in the begining I almost quit reading it, but now I'm glad I've read it, and will read more books from Sade.</span>

<div id="review_35913686" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35913686" />
    <a title="Alex Schmidt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1489185-alex-schmidt"><img alt="Alex Schmidt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1221801149p2/1489185.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35913686?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex Schmidt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex Schmidt" href="/user/show/1489185-alex-schmidt">Alex Schmidt</a>
      </span>

          is currently reading it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35913686" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9491623856213253484">Man oh man, if I ever finish this book, I will be at least 400 years old (over 1200 pages!) SO far, gruesome sexual encounters. I love the way de Sade sets up ditribes and orations. Reminds me of Plato, but with narration and scene. I like the one theme that talking can go one forever or not, yet the truth is always, and secondly, the road of excess, mainly for the imagination, can lead to the palace of wisdom.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6189880011211838633">Too much! I 'inherited' a copy of Juliette and have tried to read it several times. I have now made it (almost) to the end and whilst there are some intriguing ideas and thoughts put forward the continual sexual violence is too much for me. I had to keep putting it away (sometimes for years) before my determination to finish it took over again. Now with only a few pages left I can honestly say i'm not sure it was worth the effort!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

Interesting that this was on a suggested reading list for one of my history classes in college. Glad I only had that professor for one course.</span>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('dgC9rMXmkmQwkMl9Xk/ajlLAePpg77m6jQoh57xipNw6BAjopVxcO7rpXu70OxLoWx0s22HVZ07062gOQWXvlw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3LP5b0az43bz33vEIp58MQrcS0OTNhJatbY908rJj2yQt0wrJgktKXmm7FeI6rRXAwEfYpIMzK7MV3Q6N87EJw==')})
  }
//]]>

Lo fantástico se vuelve verosímil y lo cotidiano se hace espectral en una serie de relatos que hablan de las edades humanas desde la infancia hasta la vejez.\n\nTenga para que se entretenga es un relato ambiguo que lo mismo puede leerse como un cuento de espantos, ya transformado por la tradición oral en una más entre las muchas leyendas de la ciudad, que como una crítica de los excesos a que llega el hermano incómodo de un presidente. La zarpa explora la relación elusiva y misteriosa que llamamos amistad. La fiesta brava es acaso el primer cuento que se escribió sobre el Metro en la capital. Langerhaus contrasta la esperanza que fuimos en la niñez y la adolescencia con el fantasma en que la vida es capaz de convertirnos. Por último, Cuando salí de la Habana, válgame Dios recrea el tema ancestral de los viajes en el tiempo para demostrar que no hay escape posible contra el peso de la historia en nuestra existencia individual.\n\nAparecido por primera vez en 1972 y reeditado constantemente desde entonces, El principio del placer se ofrece ahora en una nueva versión que lo hace el mismo libro y a la vez otro nuevo, enriquecido con toda clase de precisiones que lo vuelven aún más nítido y coherente. Si innumerables lectores han disfrutado de este libro clave, su redacción final lo presenta todavía más inquietante y más abierto a otras lecturas e interpretaciones en un contexto que era impensable But the Manifesto, for all its vitriol, is impossible to dismiss as just the rantings of a lesbian lunatic. In fact, the work has indisputable prescience, not only as a radical feminist analysis light-years ahead of its timepredicting artificial insemination, ATMs, a feminist uprising against under-representation in the artsbut also as a stunning testament to the rage of an abused and destitute woman.The focus of this edition is not on the nostalgic appeal of the work, but on Avital Ronell’s incisive introduction, “Deviant Payback: The Aims of Valerie Solanas.” Here is a reconsideration of Solanas’s infamous text in light of her social milieu, Derrida’s “The Ends of Man” (written in the same year), Judith Butler’s Excitable Speech, Nietzsche’s Ubermensch and notorious feminist icons from Medusa, Medea and Antigone, to Lizzie Borden, Lorenna Bobbit and Aileen Wournos, Deleuze\'s essay, certainly the most profound study yet produced on the relations between sadism and masochism, seeks to develop and explain Masoch\'s &quot;peculiar way of \'desexualizing\' love while at the same time sexualizing the entire history of humanity.&quot; He shows that masochism is something far more subtle and complex than the enjoyment of pain, that masochism has nothing to do with sadism; their worlds do not communicate, just as the genius of those who created them - Masoch and Sade - lie stylistically, philosophically, and politically poles a part. Venus in Furs, the most famous of all of Masoch\'s novels was written in 1870 and belongs to an unfinished cycle of works that Masoch entitled The Heritage of Cain. The cycle was to treat a series of themes including love, war, and death. The present work is about love. Although the entire constellation of symbols that has come to characterize the masochistic syndrome can be found here - fetishes, whips, disguises, fur-clad women, contracts, humiliations, punishment, Each of the narratives contains a sense of intoxication and insanity so carefully delineated by the author that it seems to infect the reader.Philosopher, novelist and critic, Georges Bataille is a major figure in twentieth-century literature whose startling and original ideas increasingly exert a vital influence on the shaping of thought, language and experience. His critical essays, Literature and Evil and his complex meditations on the dark coupling of sex and death, Eroticism, are both available from Marion Boyars. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5034669583493546215">Donatien Alphonse François, Marquis de Sade (1740-1814) was a French aristocrat, revolutionary politician, philosopher, and writer famous for his libertine sexuality and lifestyle.<br/><br/>His works include novels, short stories, plays, dialogues, and political tracts; in his lifetime some were published under his own name, while others appeared anonymously and Sade denied being their author. He is best kn</span>
  <span id="freeText5034669583493546215" style="display:none">Donatien Alphonse François, Marquis de Sade (1740-1814) was a French aristocrat, revolutionary politician, philosopher, and writer famous for his libertine sexuality and lifestyle.<br /><br />His works include novels, short stories, plays, dialogues, and political tracts; in his lifetime some were published under his own name, while others appeared anonymously and Sade denied being their author. He is best known for his erotic works, which combined philosophical discourse with pornography, depicting sexual fantasies with an emphasis on violence, criminality, and blasphemy against the Catholic Church. He was a proponent of extreme freedom, unrestrained by morality, religion or law.<br />Sade was incarcerated in various prisons and in an insane asylum for about 32 years of his life; eleven years in Paris (10 of which were spent in the Bastille) a month in Conciergerie, two years in a fortress, a year in Madelonnettes, three years in Bicêtre, a year in Sainte-Pélagie, and 13 years in the Charenton asylum. During the French Revolution he was an elected delegate to the National Convention. Many of his works were written in prison.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5034669583493546215" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

Den första dygdig, den andra ond; den första ständigt bestraffad för sin godhet, den andra belönad för sin skamlöshet.\n\nMarkis de Sade (1740-1814) ägnade systrarna var sin roman. Nu kommer historien om Juliette för första gången på svenska. I denna volym - den första av tre - får vi följa hennes väg från klostret till bordellen, från livet som föräldralös till framgångsrik kurtisan i 1700-talets Paris.\n\nDen här klassiska romanen av &quot;den gudomlige markisen&quot; kokar av pornografi, filosofi och svart humor: det självklara vänds upp och ner, det normala och rimliga attackeras, det onjutbara blir njutbart. Här möts intellektet och köttet på ett svindlande sätt.\n\nMarkis de Sades Juliette eller Lastbarhetens fördelar kom 1797 i sex delar. Vertigo förlag publicerar Hans Johanssons översättning i tre volymer med originalets illustrationer och med efterord av C-M Edenborg och Hans Johansson. \n\nDet är en verklig lyxupplaga: röda linneband, trådbindning, med svart sidensnöre och skyddsomslag.\n\nHans Johansson har skrivit böcker om konstnärer som Lennart Aschen­bren­ner och Ola Billgren, medverkar i National­encyklopedin och
